border-bottom: 1px solid #aaa;">\n<p><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:046CupolaSPietro.jpg"><img alt="046CupolaSPietro.jpg" class="noviewer" data-file-height="600" data-file-width="800" decoding="async" height="12" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/046CupolaSPietro.jpg/16px-046CupolaSPietro.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/046CupolaSPietro.jpg/24px-046CupolaSPietro.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/046CupolaSPietro.jpg/32px-046CupolaSPietro.jpg 2x" width="16"/></a> <a href="/wiki/Portal:Catholicism" title="Portal:Catholicism">Catholicism portal</a>\n</p>\n<a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Socrates.png"><img alt="Socrates.png" class="noviewer" data-file-height="500" data-file-width="326" decoding="async" height="16" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/Socrates.png/10px-Socrates.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/Socrates.png/15px-Socrates.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/Socrates.png/21px-Socrates.png 2x" width="10"/></a> <a href="/wiki/Portal:Philosophy" title="Portal:Philosophy">Philosophy portal</a></td></tr><tr><td style="text-align:right;font-size:115%;padding-top: 0.6em;"><div class="plainlinks hlist navbar mini"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Catholic_philosophy" title="Template:Catholic philosophy"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Catholic_philosophy" title="Template talk:Catholic philosophy"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Catholic_philosophy&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p>\n<b>Ren&#233; Descartes</b> (<span class="nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="\'d\' in \'dye\'">d</span><span title="/e&#618;/: \'a\' in \'face\'">e&#618;</span><span title="/&#712;/: primary stress follows">&#712;</span><span title="\'k\' in \'kind\'">k</span><span title="/&#593;&#720;r/: \'ar\' in \'far\'">&#593;&#720;r</span><span title="\'t\' in \'tie\'">t</span></span>/</a></span></span>, <span style="font-size:85%;">UK also</span> <span class="nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/&#712;/: primary stress follows">&#712;</span><span title="\'d\' in \'dye\'">d</span><span title="/e&#618;/: \'a\' in \'face\'">e&#618;</span><span title="\'k\' in \'kind\'">k</span><span title="/&#593;&#720;r/: \'ar\' in \'far\'">&#593;&#720;r</span><span title="\'t\' in \'tie\'">t</span></span>/</a></span></span>;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup> <small>French:&#160;</small><span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/French" title="Help:IPA/French">[&#641;&#601;ne deka&#641;t]</a></span>; <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Latinisation_(literature)" title="Latinisation (literature)">Latinized</a>: <b>Renatus Cartesius</b>; <a href="/wiki/Adjective" title="Adjective">adjectival form</a>: "Cartesian";<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup> 31 March 1596 &#8211; 11 February 1650) was a French <a href="/wiki/Philosopher" title="Philosopher">philosopher</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mathematics" title="Mathematics">mathematician</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Scientist" title="Scientist">scientist</a>. A native of the Kingdom of France, he spent about 20 years (1629&#8211;49) of his life in the <a href="/wiki/Dutch_Republic" title="Dutch Republic">Dutch Republic</a> after serving for a while in the <a href="/wiki/Dutch_States_Army" title="Dutch States Army">Dutch States Army</a> of <a href="/wiki/Maurice,_Prince_of_Orange" title="Maurice, Prince of Orange">Maurice of Nassau</a>, <a href="/wiki/Prince_of_Orange" title="Prince of Orange">Prince of Orange</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Stadtholder" title="Stadtholder">Stadtholder</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Dutch_Republic" title="Dutch Republic">United Provinces</a>. He is generally considered one of the most notable intellectual figures of the <a href="/wiki/Dutch_Golden_Age" title="Dutch Golden Age">Dutch Golden Age</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Descartes\' <i><a href="/wiki/Meditations_on_First_Philosophy" title="Meditations on First Philosophy">Meditations on First Philosophy</a></i> (1641) continues to be a standard text at most university philosophy departments. Descartes\' influence in mathematics is equally apparent; the <a href="/wiki/Cartesian_coordinate_system" title="Cartesian coordinate system">Cartesian coordinate system</a> (see <a href="#Mathematical_legacy">below</a>) was named after him. He is credited as the father of <a href="/wiki/Analytic_geometry" title="Analytic geometry">analytical geometry</a>, the bridge between algebra and geometry, used in the discovery of infinitesimal <a href="/wiki/Calculus" title="Calculus">calculus</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mathematical_analysis" title="Mathematical analysis">analysis</a>. Descartes was also one of the key figures in the <a href="/wiki/Scientific_Revolution" title="Scientific Revolution">Scientific Revolution</a>.\n</p><p>Descartes refused to accept the authority of previous philosophers. He frequently set his views apart from those of his predecessors. In the opening section of the <i><a href="/wiki/Passions_of_the_Soul" title="Passions of the Soul">Passions of the Soul</a></i>, an <a href="/wiki/Early_modern_period" title="Early modern period">early modern</a> treatise on emotions, Descartes goes so far as to assert that he will write on this topic "as if no one had written on these matters before". His best known philosophical statement is "<a href="/wiki/Cogito,_ergo_sum" title="Cogito, ergo sum">I think, therefore I am</a>" (French: <i lang="fr">Je pense, donc je suis</i>; Latin: <i lang="la">Ego cogito, ergo sum</i>), found in <i><a href="/wiki/Discourse_on_the_Method" title="Discourse on the Method">Discourse on the Method</a></i> (1637; written in French and Latin) and <i><a href="/wiki/Principles_of_Philosophy" title="Principles of Philosophy">Principles of Philosophy</a></i> (1644; written in Latin).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Many elements of his philosophy have precedents in late <a href="/wiki/Aristotelianism" title="Aristotelianism">Aristotelianism</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Neostoicism" title="Neostoicism">revived Stoicism</a> of the 16th century, or in earlier philosophers like <a href="/wiki/Augustine_of_Hippo" title="Augustine of Hippo">Augustine</a>. In his <a href="/wiki/Natural_philosophy" title="Natural philosophy">natural philosophy</a>, he differed from the <a href="/wiki/Scholasticism" title="Scholasticism">schools</a> on two major points: first, he rejected the splitting of <a href="/wiki/Matter" title="Matter">corporeal substance</a> into matter and form; second, he rejected any appeal to <a href="/wiki/Teleology" title="Teleology">final ends</a>, divine or natural, in explaining natural phenomena.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup> In his theology, he insists on the absolute freedom of <a href="/wiki/Creationism" title="Creationism">God\'s act of creation</a>.\n</p><p>Descartes laid the foundation for 17th-century continental <a href="/wiki/Rationalism" title="Rationalism">rationalism</a>, later advocated by <a href="/wiki/Baruch_Spinoza" title="Baruch Spinoza">Spinoza</a> and <a href="/wiki/Gottfried_Wilhelm_Leibniz" title="Gottfried Wilhelm Leibniz">Leibniz</a>, and opposed by the <a href="/wiki/Empiricism" title="Empiricism">empiricist</a> school of thought consisting of <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Hobbes" title="Thomas Hobbes">Hobbes</a>, <a href="/wiki/John_Locke" title="John Locke">Locke</a>, <a href="/wiki/George_Berkeley" title="George Berkeley">Berkeley</a>, and <a href="/wiki/David_Hume" title="David Hume">Hume</a>. Leibniz, Spinoza,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup> and Descartes were all well-versed in mathematics as well as philosophy, and Descartes and Leibniz contributed greatly to science as well.\n</p>\n<div class="toc" id="toc"><input class="toctogglecheckbox" id="toctogglecheckbox" role="button" style="display:none" type="checkbox"/><div class="toctitle" dir="ltr" lang="en"><h2>Contents</h2><span class="toctogglespan"><label class="toctogglelabel" for="toctogglecheckbox"></label></span></div>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-22"><a href="#See_also"><span class="tocnumber">6</span> <span class="toctext">See also</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-23"><a href="#Notes"><span class="tocnumber">7</span> <span class="toctext">Notes</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-24"><a href="#References"><span class="tocnumber">8</span> <span class="toctext">References</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-25"><a href="#Collected_works"><span class="tocnumber">8.1</span> <span class="toctext">Collected works</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-26"><a href="#Specific_works"><span class="tocnumber">8.2</span> <span class="toctext">Specific works</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 class="tocnumber">9.1</span> <span class="toctext">General</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-32"><a href="#Bibliographies"><span class="tocnumber">9.2</span> <span class="toctext">Bibliographies</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-33"><a href="#Stanford_Encyclopedia_of_Philosophy"><span class="tocnumber">9.3</span> <span class="toctext">Stanford Encyclopedia of Philosophy</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-34"><a href="#Internet_Encyclopedia_of_Philosophy"><span class="tocnumber">9.4</span> <span class="toctext">Internet Encyclopedia of Philosophy</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-35"><a href="#Other"><span class="tocnumber">9.5</span> <span class="toctext">Other</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n</ul>\n</div>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Life">Life</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ren%C3%A9_Descartes&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Life">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_life">Early life</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ren%C3%A9_Descartes&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Early life">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Maison_de_Ren%C3%A9_DESCARTES_-_Jean-Charles_GUILLO.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2304" data-file-width="3072" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f9/Maison_de_Ren%C3%A9_DESCARTES_-_Jean-Charles_GUILLO.JPG/220px-Maison_de_Ren%C3%A9_DESCARTES_-_Jean-Charles_GUILLO.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f9/Maison_de_Ren%C3%A9_DESCARTES_-_Jean-Charles_GUILLO.JPG/330px-Maison_de_Ren%C3%A9_DESCARTES_-_Jean-Charles_GUILLO.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f9/Maison_de_Ren%C3%A9_DESCARTES_-_Jean-Charles_GUILLO.JPG/440px-Maison_de_Ren%C3%A9_DESCARTES_-_Jean-Charles_GUILLO.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Maison_de_Ren%C3%A9_DESCARTES_-_Jean-Charles_GUILLO.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The house where Descartes was born in <a href="/wiki/Descartes,_Indre-et-Loire" title="Descartes, Indre-et-Loire">La Haye en Touraine</a></div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:DescartesGraduationRegistry.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1845" data-file-width="1804" decoding="async" height="225" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/DescartesGraduationRegistry.JPG/220px-DescartesGraduationRegistry.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/DescartesGraduationRegistry.JPG/330px-DescartesGraduationRegistry.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/DescartesGraduationRegistry.JPG/440px-DescartesGraduationRegistry.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:DescartesGraduationRegistry.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Graduation registry for Descartes at the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Poitiers" title="University of Poitiers">University of Poitiers</a>, 1616</div></div></div>\n<p>Ren&#233; Descartes was born in La Haye en Touraine (now <a href="/wiki/Descartes,_Indre-et-Loire" title="Descartes, Indre-et-Loire">Descartes, Indre-et-Loire</a>), France, on 31 March 1596.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:2_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-:2-21">[21]</a></sup> His mother, Jeanne Brochard, died soon after giving birth to him, and so he was not expected to survive.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:2_21-1"><a href="#cite_note-:2-21">[21]</a></sup> Descartes\' father, Joachim, was a member of the <a href="/wiki/Parlement_of_Brittany" title="Parlement of Brittany">Parlement of Brittany</a> at <a href="/wiki/Rennes" title="Rennes">Rennes</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup> Ren&#233; lived with his grandmother and with his great-uncle. Although the Descartes family was Roman Catholic, the Poitou region was controlled by the Protestant <a href="/wiki/Huguenots" title="Huguenots">Huguenots</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup> In 1607, late because of his fragile health, he entered the <a href="/wiki/Society_of_Jesus" title="Society of Jesus">Jesuit</a> <a href="/wiki/Prytan%C3%A9e_National_Militaire" title="Prytan&#233;e National Militaire">Coll&#232;ge Royal Henry-Le-Grand</a> at <a href="/wiki/La_Fl%C3%A8che" title="La Fl&#232;che">La Fl&#232;che</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:3_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-:3-24">[24]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup> where he was introduced to mathematics and physics, including Galileo\'s work.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:3_24-1"><a href="#cite_note-:3-24">[24]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup> After graduation in 1614, he studied for two years (1615&#8211;16) at the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Poitiers" title="University of Poitiers">University of Poitiers</a>, earning a <i><a href="/wiki/Baccalaur%C3%A9at" title="Baccalaur&#233;at">Baccalaur&#233;at</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Licentiate_(degree)" title="Licentiate (degree)">Licence</a></i> in <a href="/wiki/Canon_law" title="Canon law">canon</a> and <a href="/wiki/Civil_law_(legal_system)" title="Civil law (legal system)">civil law</a> in 1616,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:3_24-2"><a href="#cite_note-:3-24">[24]</a></sup> in accordance with his father\'s wishes that he should become a lawyer.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup> From there he moved to Paris.\n</p><p>In <i><a href="/wiki/Discourse_on_the_Method" title="Discourse on the Method">Discourse on the Method</a></i>, Descartes recalls,\n</p>\n<blockquote><p>I entirely abandoned the study of letters. Resolving to seek no knowledge other than that of which could be found in myself or else in the great book of the world, I spent the rest of my youth traveling, visiting courts and armies, mixing with people of diverse temperaments and ranks, gathering various experiences, testing myself in the situations which fortune offered me, and at all times reflecting upon whatever came my way so as to derive some profit from it.</p></blockquote>\n<p>Given his ambition to become a professional military officer, in 1618, Descartes joined, as a <a href="/wiki/Mercenary" title="Mercenary">mercenary</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Protestantism" title="Protestantism">Protestant</a> <a href="/wiki/Dutch_States_Army" title="Dutch States Army">Dutch States Army</a> in Breda under the command of <a href="/wiki/Maurice,_Prince_of_Orange" title="Maurice, Prince of Orange">Maurice of Nassau</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:3_24-3"><a href="#cite_note-:3-24">[24]</a></sup> and undertook a formal study of <a href="/wiki/Military_engineering" title="Military engineering">military engineering</a>, as established by <a href="/wiki/Simon_Stevin" title="Simon Stevin">Simon Stevin</a>. Descartes, therefore, received much encouragement in Breda to advance his knowledge of mathematics.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:3_24-4"><a href="#cite_note-:3-24">[24]</a></sup> In this way, he became acquainted with <a href="/wiki/Isaac_Beeckman" title="Isaac Beeckman">Isaac Beeckman</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:3_24-5"><a href="#cite_note-:3-24">[24]</a></sup> the principal of a <a href="/wiki/Dordrecht" title="Dordrecht">Dordrecht</a> school, for whom he wrote the <i>Compendium of Music</i> (written 1618, published 1650). Together they worked on <a href="/wiki/Free_fall" title="Free fall">free fall</a>, <a href="/wiki/Catenary" title="Catenary">catenary</a>, <a href="/wiki/Conic_section" title="Conic section">conic section</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Hydrostatics" title="Hydrostatics">fluid statics</a>. Both believed that it was necessary to create a method that thoroughly linked mathematics and physics.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Durandin_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-Durandin-28">[28]</a></sup>\n</p><p>While in the service of the <a href="/wiki/Catholic_Church" title="Catholic Church">Catholic</a> Duke <a href="/wiki/Maximilian_I,_Elector_of_Bavaria" title="Maximilian I, Elector of Bavaria">Maximilian of Bavaria</a> since 1619,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup> Descartes was present at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_White_Mountain" title="Battle of White Mountain">Battle of the White Mountain</a> outside <a href="/wiki/Prague" title="Prague">Prague</a>, in November 1620.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Visions">Visions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ren%C3%A9_Descartes&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Visions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>According to <a href="/wiki/Adrien_Baillet" title="Adrien Baillet">Adrien Baillet</a>, on the night of 10&#8211;11 November 1619 (<a href="/wiki/St._Martin%27s_Day" title="St. Martin\'s Day">St. Martin\'s Day</a>), while stationed in <a href="/wiki/Neuburg_an_der_Donau" title="Neuburg an der Donau">Neuburg an der Donau</a>, Descartes shut himself in a room with an "oven" (probably a <i>Kachelofen</i> or <a href="/wiki/Masonry_heater" title="Masonry heater">masonry heater</a>) to escape the cold. While within, he had three dreams<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:4_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-:4-31">[31]</a></sup> and believed that a divine spirit revealed to him a new philosophy. However, it is likely that what Descartes considered to be his second dream was actually an episode of <a href="/wiki/Exploding_head_syndrome" title="Exploding head syndrome">exploding head syndrome</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Otaiku2018_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-Otaiku2018-32">[32]</a></sup> Upon exiting, he had formulated <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Analytical_geometry" title="Analytical geometry">analytical geometry</a> and the idea of applying the mathematical method to philosophy. He concluded from these visions that the pursuit of science would prove to be, for him, the pursuit of true wisdom and a central part of his life\'s work.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup> Descartes also saw very clearly that all truths were linked with one another so that finding a fundamental truth and proceeding with logic would open the way to all science. Descartes discovered this basic truth quite soon: his famous "<a href="/wiki/Cogito,_ergo_sum" title="Cogito, ergo sum">I think, therefore I am</a>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Durandin_28-1"><a href="#cite_note-Durandin-28">[28]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="France">France</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ren%C3%A9_Descartes&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: France">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>In 1620 Descartes left the army. He visited <a href="/wiki/Basilica_della_Santa_Casa" title="Basilica della Santa Casa">Basilica della Santa Casa</a> in Loreto, then visited various countries before returning to France, and during the next few years spent time in Paris. It was there that he composed his first essay on method: <i>Regulae ad Directionem Ingenii</i> (<a href="/wiki/Rules_for_the_Direction_of_the_Mind" title="Rules for the Direction of the Mind">Rules for the Direction of the Mind</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Durandin_28-2"><a href="#cite_note-Durandin-28">[28]</a></sup> He arrived in <a href="/wiki/The_Hague" title="The Hague">La Haye</a> in 1623, selling all of his property to invest in <a href="/wiki/Bond_(finance)" title="Bond (finance)">bonds</a>, which provided a comfortable income for the rest of his life.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup> Descartes was present at the <a href="/wiki/Siege_of_La_Rochelle" title="Siege of La Rochelle">siege of La Rochelle</a> by <a href="/wiki/Cardinal_Richelieu" title="Cardinal Richelieu">Cardinal Richelieu</a> in 1627.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup> In the fall of the same year, in the residence of the papal nuncio <a href="/wiki/Giovanni_Francesco_Guidi_di_Bagno" title="Giovanni Francesco Guidi di Bagno">Guidi di Bagno</a>, where he came with <a href="/wiki/Marin_Mersenne" title="Marin Mersenne">Mersenne</a> and many other scholars to listen to a lecture given by the alchemist Nicolas de Villiers, Sieur de Chandoux on the principles of a supposed new philosophy,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup> Cardinal <a href="/wiki/Pierre_de_B%C3%A9rulle" title="Pierre de B&#233;rulle">B&#233;rulle</a> urged him to write an exposition of his own new philosophy in some location beyond the reach of the inquisition.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Netherlands">Netherlands</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ren%C3%A9_Descartes&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Netherlands">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Westermarkt_6,_Descarteshuis_(links).JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3072" data-file-width="2304" decoding="async" height="293" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/Westermarkt_6%2C_Descarteshuis_%28links%29.JPG/220px-Westermarkt_6%2C_Descarteshuis_%28links%29.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/Westermarkt_6%2C_Descarteshuis_%28links%29.JPG/330px-Westermarkt_6%2C_Descarteshuis_%28links%29.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/Westermarkt_6%2C_Descarteshuis_%28links%29.JPG/440px-Westermarkt_6%2C_Descarteshuis_%28links%29.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Westermarkt_6,_Descarteshuis_(links).JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>In <a href="/wiki/Amsterdam" title="Amsterdam">Amsterdam</a>, Descartes lived on Westermarkt 6 (Descarteshuis, on the left).</div></div></div>\n<p>Descartes returned to the Dutch Republic in 1628.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:4_31-1"><a href="#cite_note-:4-31">[31]</a></sup> In April 1629 he joined the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Franeker" title="University of Franeker">University of Franeker</a>, studying under <a href="/wiki/Adriaan_Metius" title="Adriaan Metius">Adriaan Metius</a>, living either with a Catholic family, or renting the <a href="/wiki/Sjaerdemaslot" title="Sjaerdemaslot">Sjaerdemaslot</a>, where he invited in vain a French cook and an optician.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> The next year, under the name "Poitevin", he enrolled at the <a href="/wiki/Leiden_University" title="Leiden University">Leiden University</a> to study mathematics with <a href="/wiki/Jacobus_Golius" title="Jacobus Golius">Jacobus Golius</a>, who confronted him with <a href="/wiki/Pappus%27s_hexagon_theorem" title="Pappus\'s hexagon theorem">Pappus\'s hexagon theorem</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Astronomy" title="Astronomy">astronomy</a> with <a href="/wiki/Martin_van_den_Hove" title="Martin van den Hove">Martin Hortensius</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup> In October 1630 he had a falling-out with Beeckman, whom he accused of plagiarizing some of his ideas. In Amsterdam, he had a relationship with a servant girl, Helena Jans van der Strom, with whom he had a daughter, <a href="/wiki/Francine_Descartes" title="Francine Descartes">Francine</a>, who was born in 1635 in <a href="/wiki/Deventer" title="Deventer">Deventer</a>. She died of scarlet fever at the age of 5.\n</p><p>Unlike many moralists of the time, Descartes was not devoid of passions but rather defended them; he wept upon Francine\'s death in 1640.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup> "Descartes said that he did not believe that one must refrain from tears to prove oneself a man." <a href="/wiki/Russell_Shorto" title="Russell Shorto">Russell Shorto</a> postulated that the experience of fatherhood and losing a child formed a turning point in Descartes\' work, changing its focus from medicine to a quest for universal answers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Despite frequent moves,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup> he wrote all his major work during his 20-plus years in the Netherlands, where he managed to revolutionize mathematics and philosophy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup> In 1633, <a href="/wiki/Galileo_Galilei" title="Galileo Galilei">Galileo</a> was condemned by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Italian_Inquisition" title="Italian Inquisition">Italian Inquisition</a>, and Descartes abandoned plans to publish <i><a href="/wiki/The_World_(Descartes)" title="The World (Descartes)">Treatise on the World</a></i>, his work of the previous four years. Nevertheless, in 1637 he published part of this work<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:5_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-:5-44">[44]</a></sup> in three essays: "Les M&#233;t&#233;ores" (The Meteors), "<a href="/wiki/Dioptrique" title="Dioptrique">La Dioptrique</a>" (Dioptrics) and "La G&#233;om&#233;trie" (Geometry), preceded by an introduction, his famous <i>Discours de la m&#233;thode</i> (Discourse on the Method).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:5_44-1"><a href="#cite_note-:5-44">[44]</a></sup> In it, Descartes lays out four rules of thought, meant to ensure that our knowledge rests upon a firm foundation.\n</p>\n<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r856303468">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>The first was never to accept anything for true which I did not clearly know to be such; that is to say, carefully to avoid precipitancy and prejudice, and to comprise nothing more in my judgment than what was presented to my mind so clearly and distinctly as to exclude all ground of doubt.\n</p></blockquote><p>In <i>La G&#233;om&#233;trie</i>, Descartes exploited the discoveries he made with <a href="/wiki/Pierre_de_Fermat" title="Pierre de Fermat">Pierre de Fermat</a>, having been able to do so because his paper, Introduction to Loci, was published posthumously in 1679.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:6_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-:6-45">[45]</a></sup> This later became known as Cartesian Geometry.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:6_45-1"><a href="#cite_note-:6-45">[45]</a></sup> </p><div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ren%C3%A9_Descartes_1644_Principia_philosophiae.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3440" data-file-width="2796" decoding="async" height="271" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e3/Ren%C3%A9_Descartes_1644_Principia_philosophiae.jpg/220px-Ren%C3%A9_Descartes_1644_Principia_philosophiae.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e3/Ren%C3%A9_Descartes_1644_Principia_philosophiae.jpg/330px-Ren%C3%A9_Descartes_1644_Principia_philosophiae.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e3/Ren%C3%A9_Descartes_1644_Principia_philosophiae.jpg/440px-Ren%C3%A9_Descartes_1644_Principia_philosophiae.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ren%C3%A9_Descartes_1644_Principia_philosophiae.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>Principia philosophiae</i>, 1644</div></div></div>\n<p>Descartes continued to publish works concerning both mathematics and philosophy for the rest of his life. In 1641 he published a metaphysics work, <i>Meditationes de Prima Philosophia</i> (Meditations on First Philosophy), written in Latin and thus addressed to the learned. It was followed, in 1644, by <i>Principia Philosophi&#230;</i> (Principles of Philosophy), a kind of synthesis of the <i>Discourse on the Method</i> and <i>Meditations on First Philosophy</i>. In 1643, Cartesian philosophy was condemned at the University of Utrecht, and Descartes was obliged to flee to the Hague, and settled in <a href="/wiki/Egmond-Binnen" title="Egmond-Binnen">Egmond-Binnen</a>.\n</p><p>Descartes began (through Alfonso Polloti, an Italian general in Dutch service) a long correspondence with <a href="/wiki/Elisabeth_of_the_Palatinate" title="Elisabeth of the Palatinate">Princess Elisabeth of Bohemia</a>, devoted mainly to moral and psychological subjects. Connected with this correspondence, in 1649 he published <i>Les Passions de l\'&#226;me</i> (Passions of the Soul), that he dedicated to the Princess. In 1647, he was awarded a pension by King <a href="/wiki/Louis_XIV_of_France" title="Louis XIV of France">Louis XIV of France</a>, though it was never paid.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Descartes,_Ren&#233;_2009_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-Descartes,_Ren&#233;_2009-46">[46]</a></sup> A French translation of <i>Principia Philosophi&#230;</i>, prepared by Abbot Claude Picot, was published in 1647. This edition Descartes also dedicated to Princess Elisabeth. In <a href="/wiki/Principles_of_Philosophy#Preface_to_the_French_edition" title="Principles of Philosophy">the preface to the French edition</a>, Descartes praised true philosophy as a means to attain wisdom. He identifies four ordinary sources to reach wisdom and finally says that there is a fifth, better and more secure, consisting in the search for first causes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Sweden">Sweden</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ren%C3%A9_Descartes&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Sweden">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ren%C3%A9_Descartes_i_samtal_med_Sveriges_drottning,_Kristina.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="539" data-file-width="581" decoding="async" height="204" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/40/Ren%C3%A9_Descartes_i_samtal_med_Sveriges_drottning%2C_Kristina.jpg/220px-Ren%C3%A9_Descartes_i_samtal_med_Sveriges_drottning%2C_Kristina.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/40/Ren%C3%A9_Descartes_i_samtal_med_Sveriges_drottning%2C_Kristina.jpg/330px-Ren%C3%A9_Descartes_i_samtal_med_Sveriges_drottning%2C_Kristina.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/40/Ren%C3%A9_Descartes_i_samtal_med_Sveriges_drottning%2C_Kristina.jpg/440px-Ren%C3%A9_Descartes_i_samtal_med_Sveriges_drottning%2C_Kristina.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ren%C3%A9_Descartes_i_samtal_med_Sveriges_drottning,_Kristina.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Ren&#233; Descartes (right) with <a href="/wiki/Christina,_Queen_of_Sweden" title="Christina, Queen of Sweden">Queen Christina of Sweden</a> (left)</div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Vasterlanggatan_68_portal_March_2007.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1280" data-file-width="1024" decoding="async" height="275" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/Vasterlanggatan_68_portal_March_2007.JPG/220px-Vasterlanggatan_68_portal_March_2007.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/Vasterlanggatan_68_portal_March_2007.JPG/330px-Vasterlanggatan_68_portal_March_2007.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/Vasterlanggatan_68_portal_March_2007.JPG/440px-Vasterlanggatan_68_portal_March_2007.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Vasterlanggatan_68_portal_March_2007.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The rear of the "von der Lindeska huset" on V&#228;sterl&#229;nggatan 68</div></div></div>\n<p>By 1649, Descartes had become famous throughout Europe for being one of the continent\'s greatest philosophers and scientists.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:5_44-2"><a href="#cite_note-:5-44">[44]</a></sup> That year, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Queen_Christina_of_Sweden" title="Queen Christina of Sweden">Queen Christina of Sweden</a> invited Descartes to her court to organize a new scientific academy and tutor her in his ideas about love. She was interested in and stimulated Descartes to publish the "<a href="/wiki/Passions_of_the_Soul" title="Passions of the Soul">Passions of the Soul</a>", a work based on his correspondence with Princess Elisabeth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Smith_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-Smith-48">[48]</a></sup> Descartes accepted, and moved to Sweden in the middle of winter.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup>\n</p><p>He was a guest at the house of <a href="/wiki/Pierre_Chanut" title="Pierre Chanut">Pierre Chanut</a>, living on <a href="/wiki/V%C3%A4sterl%C3%A5nggatan" title="V&#228;sterl&#229;nggatan">V&#228;sterl&#229;nggatan</a>, less than 500 meters from <a href="/wiki/Tre_Kronor_(castle)" title="Tre Kronor (castle)">Tre Kronor</a> in <a href="/wiki/Stockholm" title="Stockholm">Stockholm</a>. There, Chanut and Descartes made observations with a <a href="/wiki/Evangelista_Torricelli" title="Evangelista Torricelli">Torricellian</a> barometer, a tube with <a href="/wiki/Mercury_(element)" title="Mercury (element)">mercury</a>. Challenging <a href="/wiki/Blaise_Pascal" title="Blaise Pascal">Blaise Pascal</a>, Descartes took the first set of barometric readings in Stockholm to see if <a href="/wiki/Atmospheric_pressure" title="Atmospheric pressure">atmospheric pressure</a> could be used in forecasting the weather.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Death">Death</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ren%C3%A9_Descartes&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Death">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:DescartesAshes.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1843" data-file-width="2218" decoding="async" height="183" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ae/DescartesAshes.jpg/220px-DescartesAshes.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ae/DescartesAshes.jpg/330px-DescartesAshes.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ae/DescartesAshes.jpg/440px-DescartesAshes.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:DescartesAshes.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The tomb of Descartes (middle, with detail of the inscription), in the <a href="/wiki/Abbey_of_Saint-Germain-des-Pr%C3%A9s" title="Abbey of Saint-Germain-des-Pr&#233;s">Abbey of Saint-Germain-des-Pr&#233;s</a>, Paris</div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Rene_Descartes_monument_in_the_Adolf_Fredriks_Kyrka_Stockholm_2.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2592" data-file-width="1944" decoding="async" height="293" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/Rene_Descartes_monument_in_the_Adolf_Fredriks_Kyrka_Stockholm_2.jpg/220px-Rene_Descartes_monument_in_the_Adolf_Fredriks_Kyrka_Stockholm_2.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/Rene_Descartes_monument_in_the_Adolf_Fredriks_Kyrka_Stockholm_2.jpg/330px-Rene_Descartes_monument_in_the_Adolf_Fredriks_Kyrka_Stockholm_2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/Rene_Descartes_monument_in_the_Adolf_Fredriks_Kyrka_Stockholm_2.jpg/440px-Rene_Descartes_monument_in_the_Adolf_Fredriks_Kyrka_Stockholm_2.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Rene_Descartes_monument_in_the_Adolf_Fredriks_Kyrka_Stockholm_2.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>His memorial, erected in the 1720s, in the Adolf Fredriks kyrka</div></div></div>\n<p>Descartes apparently started giving lessons to Queen Christina after her birthday, three times a week, at 5 a.m, in her cold and draughty castle. Soon it became clear they did not like each other; she did not like his <a href="/wiki/Mechanical_philosophy#Descartes_and_the_mechanical_philosophy" title="Mechanical philosophy">mechanical philosophy</a>, nor did he appreciate her interest in <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek" title="Ancient Greek">Ancient Greek</a>.  By 15 January 1650, Descartes had seen Christina only four or five times. On 1 February he contracted <a href="/wiki/Pneumonia" title="Pneumonia">pneumonia</a> and died on 11 February.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup> The cause of death was pneumonia according to Chanut, but <a href="/wiki/Pleurisy" title="Pleurisy">peripneumonia</a> according to the doctor Van Wullen who was not allowed to bleed him.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup> (The winter seems to have been mild,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup> except for the second half of January which was harsh as described by Descartes himself; however, "this remark was probably intended to be as much Descartes\' take on the intellectual climate as it was about the weather."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Smith_48-1"><a href="#cite_note-Smith-48">[48]</a></sup>)\n</p><p>In 1996 E. Pies, a German scholar, published a book questioning this account, based on a letter by Johann van Wullen, who had been sent by Christina to treat him, something Descartes refused, and more arguments against its veracity have been raised since.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup> Descartes might have been assassinated<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-stockholmnews.com_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-stockholmnews.com-57">[57]</a></sup> as he asked for an <a href="/wiki/Vomiting" title="Vomiting">emetic</a>: wine mixed with tobacco.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Accuracy_dispute#Disputed_statement" title="Wikipedia:Accuracy dispute"><span title="The material near this tag is possibly inaccurate or nonfactual. (October 2017)">dubious</span></a> <span class="metadata"> &#8211; <a href="/wiki/Talk:Ren%C3%A9_Descartes#Dubious" title="Talk:Ren&#233; Descartes">discuss</a></span></i>]</sup>\n</p><p>As a Catholic<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-books.google.be_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-books.google.be-59">[59]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-KrR-5EKLSQMC_p._207_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-KrR-5EKLSQMC_p._207-60">[60]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gh_BAAAQBAJ_p._107_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gh_BAAAQBAJ_p._107-61">[61]</a></sup> in a Protestant nation, he was interred in a graveyard used mainly for orphans in <a href="/wiki/Adolf_Fredrik_Church" title="Adolf Fredrik Church">Adolf Fredriks kyrka</a> in Stockholm. His manuscripts came into the possession of <a href="/wiki/Claude_Clerselier" title="Claude Clerselier">Claude Clerselier</a>, Chanut\'s brother-in-law, and "a devout Catholic who has begun the process of turning Descartes into a saint by cutting, adding and publishing his letters selectively."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup> In 1663, the <a href="/wiki/Pope_Alexander_VII" title="Pope Alexander VII">Pope</a> placed his works on the <a href="/wiki/Index_Librorum_Prohibitorum" title="Index Librorum Prohibitorum">Index of Prohibited Books</a>. In 1666 his remains were taken to France and buried in the <a href="/wiki/Saint-%C3%89tienne-du-Mont" title="Saint-&#201;tienne-du-Mont">Saint-&#201;tienne-du-Mont</a>. In 1671 Louis XIV prohibited all the lectures in <a href="/wiki/Cartesianism" title="Cartesianism">Cartesianism</a>. Although the <a href="/wiki/National_Convention" title="National Convention">National Convention</a> in 1792 had planned to transfer his remains to the <a href="/wiki/Panth%C3%A9on" title="Panth&#233;on">Panth&#233;on</a>, he was reburied in the <a href="/wiki/Abbey_of_Saint-Germain-des-Pr%C3%A9s" title="Abbey of Saint-Germain-des-Pr&#233;s">Abbey of Saint-Germain-des-Pr&#233;s</a> in 1819, missing a finger and the skull.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup> His skull is on display in the Mus&#233;e de l\'Homme in Paris.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Philosophical_work">Philosophical work</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ren%C3%A9_Descartes&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Philosophical work">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Cartesianism" title="Cartesianism">Cartesianism</a></div>\n<p>Initially, Descartes arrives at only a single first principle: I think. Thought cannot be separated from me, therefore, I exist (<i>Discourse on the Method</i> and <i>Principles of Philosophy</i>). Most famously, this is known as <i>cogito ergo sum</i> (English: "I think, therefore I am"). Therefore, Descartes concluded, if he doubted, then something or someone must be doing the doubting, therefore the very fact that he doubted proved his existence.  "The simple meaning of the phrase is that if one is skeptical of existence, that is in and of itself proof that he does exist."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup> These two first principles&#8212;I think and I exist&#8212;were later confirmed by Descartes\'s clear and distinct perception (delineated in his Third Meditation): that I clearly and distinctly perceive these two principles, Descartes reasoned, ensures their indubitability.\n</p><p>Descartes concludes that he can be certain that he exists because he thinks. But in what form? He perceives his body through the use of the senses; however, these have previously been unreliable. So Descartes determines that the only indubitable knowledge is that he is a <i>thinking thing</i>. Thinking is what he does, and his power must come from his essence. Descartes defines "thought" (<i>cogitatio</i>) as "what happens in me such that I am immediately conscious of it, insofar as I am conscious of it". Thinking is thus every activity of a person of which the person is immediately <a href="/wiki/Consciousness" title="Consciousness">conscious</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup> He gave reasons for thinking that waking thoughts are distinguishable from <a href="/wiki/Dream_argument" title="Dream argument">dreams</a>, and that one\'s mind cannot have been "hijacked" by an <a href="/wiki/Evil_demon" title="Evil demon">evil demon</a> placing an illusory external world before one\'s senses.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-:0-67">[67]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r856303468" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>And so something that I thought I was seeing with my eyes is in fact grasped solely by the faculty of judgment which is in my mind.\n</p></blockquote>\n<p>In this manner, Descartes proceeds to construct a system of knowledge, discarding <a href="/wiki/Perception" title="Perception">perception</a> as unreliable and, instead, admitting only <a href="/wiki/Deductive_reasoning" title="Deductive reasoning">deduction</a> as a method.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Preface_68-0"><a href="#cite_note-Preface-68">[68]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Dualism">Dualism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ren%C3%A9_Descartes&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Dualism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mind-body_problem" title="Mind-body problem">Mind-body problem</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mind-body_dualism" title="Mind-body dualism">Mind-body dualism</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:L%27homme_V00083_00000004.tif"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3471" data-file-width="2606" decoding="async" height="293" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7a/L%27homme_V00083_00000004.tif/lossy-page1-220px-L%27homme_V00083_00000004.tif.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7a/L%27homme_V00083_00000004.tif/lossy-page1-330px-L%27homme_V00083_00000004.tif.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7a/L%27homme_V00083_00000004.tif/lossy-page1-440px-L%27homme_V00083_00000004.tif.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:L%27homme_V00083_00000004.tif" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>L\'homme</i> (1664)</div></div></div>\n<p>Descartes, influenced by the <a href="/wiki/Automaton" title="Automaton">automatons</a> on display throughout the city of Paris, began to investigate the connection between the mind and body, and how the two interact.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup> His main influences for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mind-body_dualism" title="Mind-body dualism">dualism</a> were <a href="/wiki/Theology" title="Theology">theology</a> and <a href="/wiki/Physics" title="Physics">physics</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup> The theory on the dualism of mind and body is Descartes\' signature doctrine and permeates other theories he advanced. Known as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cartesian_dualism" title="Cartesian dualism">Cartesian dualism</a> (or Mind-Body Dualism), his theory on the separation between the mind and the body went on to influence subsequent Western philosophies. In <i><a href="/wiki/Meditations_on_First_Philosophy" title="Meditations on First Philosophy">Meditations on First Philosophy</a></i>, Descartes attempted to demonstrate the existence of <a href="/wiki/God" title="God">God</a> and the distinction between the human soul and the body. Humans are a union of mind and body;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup> thus Descartes\' dualism embraced the idea that mind and body are distinct but closely joined. While many contemporary readers of Descartes found the distinction between mind and body difficult to grasp, he thought it was entirely straightforward. Descartes employed the concept of <i>modes</i>, which are the ways in which substances exist. In <i><a href="/wiki/Principles_of_Philosophy" title="Principles of Philosophy">Principles of Philosophy</a></i>, Descartes explained, "we can clearly perceive a substance apart from the mode which we say differs from it, whereas we cannot, conversely, understand the mode apart from the substance". To perceive a mode apart from its substance requires an intellectual abstraction,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-David_Cunning_2014_278_72-0"><a href="#cite_note-David_Cunning_2014_278-72">[72]</a></sup> which Descartes explained as follows:\n</p>\n<blockquote><p>The intellectual abstraction consists in my turning my thought away from one part of the contents of this richer idea the better to apply it to the other part with greater attention. Thus, when I consider a shape without thinking of the substance or the extension whose shape it is, I make a mental abstraction.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-David_Cunning_2014_278_72-1"><a href="#cite_note-David_Cunning_2014_278-72">[72]</a></sup>\n</p></blockquote>\n<p>According to Descartes two substances are really distinct when each of them can exist apart from the other. Thus Descartes reasoned that God is distinct from humans, and the body and mind of a human are also distinct from one another.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup> He argued that the great differences between body (an extended thing) and mind (an un-extended, immaterial thing) make the two ontologically distinct. But that the mind was utterly indivisible: because "when I consider the mind, or myself in so far as I am merely a thinking thing, I am unable to distinguish any part within myself; I understand myself to be something quite single and complete."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In <i>Meditations</i> Descartes discussed a piece of <a href="/wiki/Wax" title="Wax">wax</a> and exposed the single most characteristic doctrine of Cartesian dualism: that the universe contained two radically different kinds of substances&#8212;the mind or soul defined as <a href="/wiki/Thought" title="Thought">thinking</a>, and the body defined as matter and unthinking.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Aristotelianism" title="Aristotelianism">Aristotelian philosophy</a> of Descartes\' days held that the universe was inherently purposeful or theological. Everything that happened, be it the motion of the <a href="/wiki/Star" title="Star">stars</a> or the growth of a <a href="/wiki/Tree" title="Tree">tree</a>, was supposedly explainable by a certain purpose, goal or end that worked its way out within nature. Aristotle called this the "final cause", and these final causes were indispensable for explaining the ways nature operated. With his theory on dualism Descartes fired the opening shot for the battle between the traditional Aristotelian science and the new science of <a href="/wiki/Johannes_Kepler" title="Johannes Kepler">Kepler</a> and <a href="/wiki/Galileo_Galilei" title="Galileo Galilei">Galileo</a> which denied the final cause for explaining nature. Descartes\' dualism provided the philosophical rationale for the latter and he expelled the final cause from the physical universe (or <i>res extensa</i>). For Descartes the only place left for the final cause was the mind (or <i>res cogitans</i>). Therefore, while Cartesian dualism paved the way for modern <a href="/wiki/Physics" title="Physics">physics</a>, it also held the door open for religious beliefs about the immortality of the <a href="/wiki/Soul" title="Soul">soul</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Descartes\' dualism of mind and matter implied a concept of human beings. A human was according to Descartes a composite entity of mind and body. Descartes gave priority to the mind and argued that the mind could exist without the body, but the body could not exist without the mind. In <i>Meditations</i> Descartes even argues that while the mind is a substance, the body is composed only of "accidents".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup> But he did argue that mind and body are closely joined:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Georges_Dicker_2013_303_78-0"><a href="#cite_note-Georges_Dicker_2013_303-78">[78]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<blockquote><p>Nature also teaches me, by the sensations of pain, hunger, thirst and so on, that I am not merely present in my body as a pilot in his ship, but that I am very closely joined and, as it were, intermingled with it, so that I and the body form a unit. If this were not so, I, who am nothing but a thinking thing, would not feel pain when the body was hurt, but would perceive the damage purely by the intellect, just as a sailor perceives by sight if anything in his ship is broken.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Georges_Dicker_2013_303_78-1"><a href="#cite_note-Georges_Dicker_2013_303-78">[78]</a></sup>\n</p></blockquote>\n<p>Descartes\' discussion on embodiment raised one of the most perplexing problems of his dualism philosophy: What exactly is the relationship of union between the mind and the body of a person?<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Georges_Dicker_2013_303_78-2"><a href="#cite_note-Georges_Dicker_2013_303-78">[78]</a></sup> Therefore, Cartesian dualism set the agenda for philosophical discussion of the <a href="/wiki/Mind%E2%80%93body_problem" title="Mind&#8211;body problem">mind&#8211;body problem</a> for many years after Descartes\' death.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup> Descartes was also a <a href="/wiki/Rationalism" title="Rationalism">rationalist</a> and believed in the power of <a href="/wiki/Innatism" title="Innatism">innate ideas</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Eric_Shiraev_2010_88_80-0"><a href="#cite_note-Eric_Shiraev_2010_88-80">[80]</a></sup> Descartes argued the theory of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Innate_knowledge" title="Innate knowledge">innate knowledge</a> and that all humans were born with knowledge through the higher power of God. It was this theory of innate knowledge that later led philosopher <a href="/wiki/John_Locke" title="John Locke">John Locke</a> (1632&#8211;1704) to combat the theory of <a href="/wiki/Empiricism" title="Empiricism">empiricism</a>, which held that all knowledge is acquired through experience.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Descartes_on_physiology_and_psychology">Descartes on physiology and psychology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ren%C3%A9_Descartes&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Descartes on physiology and psychology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>In <i>The Passions of the Soul</i> written between 1645 and 1646 Descartes discussed the common contemporary belief that the human body contained animal spirits. These animal spirits were believed to be light and roaming fluids circulating rapidly around the nervous system between the brain and the muscles, and served as a metaphor for feelings, like being in high or bad spirit. These animal spirits were believed to affect the human soul, or <a href="/wiki/Passions" title="Passions">passions</a> of the soul. Descartes distinguished six basic passions: wonder, love, hatred, desire, joy and sadness. All of these passions, he argued, represented different combinations of the original spirit, and influenced the soul to will or want certain actions. He argued, for example, that fear is a passion that moves the soul to generate a response in the body. In line with his dualist teachings on the separation between the soul and the body, he hypothesized that some part of the brain served as a connector between the soul and the body and singled out the <a href="/wiki/Pineal_gland" title="Pineal gland">pineal gland</a> as connector.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup> Descartes argued that signals passed from the ear and the eye to the pineal gland, through animal spirits. Thus different motions in the gland cause various animal spirits. He argued that these motions in the pineal gland are based on God\'s will and that humans are supposed to want and like things that are useful to them. But he also argued that the animal spirits that moved around the body could distort the commands from the pineal gland, thus humans had to learn how to control their passions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Eric_Shiraev_2010_87_83-0"><a href="#cite_note-Eric_Shiraev_2010_87-83">[83]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Descartes advanced a theory on automatic bodily reactions to external events which influenced 19th-century <a href="/wiki/Reflex" title="Reflex">reflex</a> theory. He argued that external motions such as touch and sound reach the endings of the nerves and affect the animal spirits. Heat from fire affects a spot on the skin and sets in motion a chain of reactions, with the animal spirits reaching the brain through the central nervous system, and in turn animal spirits are sent back to the muscles to move the hand away from the fire.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Eric_Shiraev_2010_87_83-1"><a href="#cite_note-Eric_Shiraev_2010_87-83">[83]</a></sup> Through this chain of reactions the automatic reactions of the body do not require a thought process.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Eric_Shiraev_2010_88_80-1"><a href="#cite_note-Eric_Shiraev_2010_88-80">[80]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Above all he was among the first scientists who believed that the soul should be subject to scientific investigation. He challenged the views of his contemporaries that the soul was <a href="/wiki/Divinity" title="Divinity">divine</a>, thus religious authorities regarded his books as dangerous. Descartes\' writings went on to form the basis for theories on <a href="/wiki/Emotion" title="Emotion">emotions</a> and how <a href="/wiki/Cognition" title="Cognition">cognitive</a> evaluations were translated into affective processes. Descartes believed that the brain resembled a working machine and unlike many of his contemporaries believed that mathematics and mechanics could explain the most complicated processes of the mind. In the 20th century <a href="/wiki/Alan_Turing" title="Alan Turing">Alan Turing</a> advanced <a href="/wiki/Computer_science" title="Computer science">computer science</a> based on <a href="/wiki/Mathematical_and_theoretical_biology" title="Mathematical and theoretical biology">mathematical biology</a> as inspired by Descartes. His theories on reflexes also served as the foundation for advanced <a href="/wiki/Physiology" title="Physiology">physiological theories</a> more than 200 years after his death. The physiologist <a href="/wiki/Ivan_Pavlov" title="Ivan Pavlov">Ivan Pavlov</a> was a great admirer of Descartes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span id="Descartes.27_moral_philosophy"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Descartes\'_moral_philosophy">Descartes\' moral philosophy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ren%C3%A9_Descartes&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Descartes\' moral philosophy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>For Descartes, <a href="/wiki/Ethics" title="Ethics">ethics</a> was a science, the highest and most perfect of them. Like the rest of the sciences, ethics had its roots in metaphysics.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Preface_68-1"><a href="#cite_note-Preface-68">[68]</a></sup> In this way, he argues for the existence of God, investigates the place of man in nature, formulates the theory of mind-body dualism, and defends <a href="/wiki/Free_will" title="Free will">free will</a>. However, as he was a convinced rationalist, Descartes clearly states that reason is sufficient in the search for the goods that we should seek, and <a href="/wiki/Virtue" title="Virtue">virtue</a> consists in the correct reasoning that should guide our actions. Nevertheless, the quality of this reasoning depends on knowledge, because a well-informed mind will be more capable of making good <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Choices" title="Choices">choices</a>, and it also depends on mental condition. For this reason, he said that a complete moral philosophy should include the study of the body. He discussed this subject in the correspondence with <a href="/wiki/Elisabeth_of_the_Palatinate" title="Elisabeth of the Palatinate">Princess Elisabeth of Bohemia</a>, and as a result wrote his work <i>The Passions of the Soul</i>, that contains a study of the <a href="/wiki/Psychosomatic_medicine" title="Psychosomatic medicine">psychosomatic</a> processes and reactions in man, with an emphasis on emotions or passions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Blom_85-0"><a href="#cite_note-Blom-85">[85]</a></sup> His works about human passion and emotion would be the basis for the philosophy of his followers (see <a href="/wiki/Cartesianism" title="Cartesianism">Cartesianism</a>), and would have a lasting impact on ideas concerning what literature and art should be, specifically how it should invoke emotion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Humans should seek the <a href="/wiki/Summum_bonum" title="Summum bonum">sovereign good</a> that Descartes, following <a href="/wiki/Zeno_of_Citium" title="Zeno of Citium">Zeno</a>, identifies with virtue, as this produces a solid <a href="/wiki/Blessing" title="Blessing">blessedness</a> or <a href="/wiki/Pleasure" title="Pleasure">pleasure</a>. For <a href="/wiki/Epicurus" title="Epicurus">Epicurus</a> the sovereign good was pleasure, and Descartes says that, in fact, this is not in contradiction with Zeno\'s teaching, because virtue produces a spiritual pleasure, that is better than bodily pleasure. Regarding <a href="/wiki/Aristotle" title="Aristotle">Aristotle</a>\'s opinion that happiness depends on the goods of fortune, Descartes does not deny that this good contributes to happiness but remarks that they are in great proportion outside one\'s own control, whereas one\'s mind is under one\'s complete control.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Blom_85-1"><a href="#cite_note-Blom-85">[85]</a></sup> The moral writings of Descartes came at the last part of his life, but earlier, in his <i>Discourse on the Method</i> he adopted three maxims to be able to act while he put all his ideas into doubt. This is known as his <a href="/wiki/Discourse_on_the_Method#Part_III:_Morals,_and_Maxims_accepted_while_conducting_Method" title="Discourse on the Method">"Provisional Morals"</a>.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Descartes_on_religious_beliefs">Descartes on religious beliefs</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ren%C3%A9_Descartes&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Descartes on religious beliefs">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Descartes3.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="660" data-file-width="457" decoding="async" height="318" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/Descartes3.jpg/220px-Descartes3.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/Descartes3.jpg/330px-Descartes3.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/Descartes3.jpg/440px-Descartes3.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Descartes3.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Ren&#233; Descartes at work</div></div></div>\n<p>In the third and fifth <i>Meditation</i>, he offers an <a href="/wiki/Ontological_argument" title="Ontological argument">ontological proof</a> of a benevolent God (through both the ontological argument and <a href="/wiki/Trademark_argument" title="Trademark argument">trademark argument</a>). Because God is benevolent, he can have some faith in the account of reality his senses provide him, for God has provided him with a working mind and <a href="/wiki/Sensory_nervous_system" title="Sensory nervous system">sensory system</a> and does not desire to deceive him. From this supposition, however, he finally establishes the possibility of acquiring knowledge about the world based on deduction <i>and</i> perception. Regarding <a href="/wiki/Epistemology" title="Epistemology">epistemology</a>, therefore, he can be said to have contributed such ideas as a rigorous conception of <a href="/wiki/Foundationalism" title="Foundationalism">foundationalism</a> and the possibility that <a href="/wiki/Reason" title="Reason">reason</a> is the only reliable method of attaining knowledge. He, nevertheless, was very much aware that experimentation was necessary to verify and validate theories.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Preface_68-2"><a href="#cite_note-Preface-68">[68]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In his <i>Meditations on First Philosophy</i> Descartes sets forth two proofs for God\'s existence. One of these is founded upon the possibility of thinking the "idea of a being that is supremely perfect and infinite," and suggests that "of all the ideas that are in me, the idea that I have of God is the most true, the most clear and distinct."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup> Descartes considered himself to be a devout Catholic<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-books.google.be_59-1"><a href="#cite_note-books.google.be-59">[59]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-KrR-5EKLSQMC_p._207_60-1"><a href="#cite_note-KrR-5EKLSQMC_p._207-60">[60]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gh_BAAAQBAJ_p._107_61-1"><a href="#cite_note-Gh_BAAAQBAJ_p._107-61">[61]</a></sup> and one of the purposes of the <i>Meditations</i> was to defend the Catholic faith. His attempt to ground theological beliefs on reason encountered intense opposition in his time, however: <a href="/wiki/Blaise_Pascal" title="Blaise Pascal">Pascal</a> regarded Descartes\' views as rationalist and mechanist, and accused him of <a href="/wiki/Deism" title="Deism">deism</a>: "I cannot forgive Descartes; in all his philosophy, Descartes did his best to dispense with God. But Descartes could not avoid prodding God to set the world in motion with a snap of his lordly fingers; after that, he had no more use for God," while a powerful contemporary, <a href="/wiki/Martin_Schoock" title="Martin Schoock">Martin Schoock</a>, accused him of <a href="/wiki/Atheism" title="Atheism">atheist</a> beliefs, though Descartes had provided an explicit critique of atheism in his <i>Meditations</i>. The Catholic Church prohibited his books in 1663.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Descartes,_Ren&#233;_2009_46-1"><a href="#cite_note-Descartes,_Ren&#233;_2009-46">[46]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup>\n<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style#Punctuation" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style"><span title="Quotation marks may be incomplete, unbalanced or improperly applied.">check quotation syntax</span></a></i>]</sup>\n</p><p>Descartes also wrote a response to <a href="/wiki/Philosophical_skepticism" title="Philosophical skepticism">external world scepticism</a>. Through this method of scepticism, he does not doubt for the sake of doubting but to achieve concrete and reliable information. In other words, certainty. He argues that sensory <a href="/wiki/Perception" title="Perception">perceptions</a> come to him involuntarily, and are not willed by him. They are external to his senses, and according to Descartes, this is evidence of the existence of something outside of his mind, and thus, an external world. Descartes goes on to show that the things in the external world are material by arguing that God would not deceive him as to the ideas that are being transmitted, and that God has given him the "propensity" to believe that such ideas are caused by material things. Descartes also believes a substance is something that does not need any assistance to function or exist. Descartes further explains how only God can be a true &#8220;substance&#8221;. But minds are substances, meaning they need only God for it to function. The mind is a thinking substance. The means for a thinking substance stem from ideas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_89-0"><a href="#cite_note-:1-89">[89]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Descartes_and_natural_science">Descartes and natural science</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ren%C3%A9_Descartes&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Descartes and natural science">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Descartes is often regarded as the first thinker to emphasize the use of reason to develop the <a href="/wiki/Natural_science" title="Natural science">natural sciences</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup> For him the philosophy was a thinking system that embodied all knowledge, and expressed it in this way:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Preface_68-3"><a href="#cite_note-Preface-68">[68]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r856303468" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>Thus, all Philosophy is like a tree, of which Metaphysics is the root, Physics the trunk, and all the other sciences the branches that grow out of this trunk, which are reduced to three principals, namely, Medicine, Mechanics, and Ethics. By the science of Morals, I understand the highest and most perfect which, presupposing an entire knowledge of the other sciences, is the last degree of wisdom.\n</p></blockquote>\n<p>In his <i>Discourse on the Method</i>, he attempts to arrive at a fundamental set of principles that one can know as true without any doubt. To achieve this, he employs a method called hyperbolical/metaphysical doubt, also sometimes referred to as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Methodological_scepticism" title="Methodological scepticism">methodological scepticism</a>: he rejects any ideas that can be doubted and then re-establishes them in order to acquire a firm foundation for genuine knowledge.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup> Descartes built his ideas from scratch. He relates this to architecture: the top soil is taken away to create a new building or structure. Descartes calls his doubt the soil and new knowledge the buildings. To Descartes, Aristotle\'s foundationalism is incomplete and his method of doubt enhances foundationalism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_67-1"><a href="#cite_note-:0-67">[67]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Descartes_on_animals">Descartes on animals</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ren%C3%A9_Descartes&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Descartes on animals">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Descartes denied that animals had reason or intelligence. He argued that animals did not lack sensations or perceptions, but these could be explained mechanistically.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[92]</a></sup> Whereas humans had a soul, or mind, and were able to feel <a href="/wiki/Pain" title="Pain">pain</a> and <a href="/wiki/Anxiety" title="Anxiety">anxiety</a>, animals by virtue of not having a soul could not feel pain or anxiety. If animals showed signs of distress then this was to protect the body from damage, but the innate state needed for them to <a href="/wiki/Suffering" title="Suffering">suffer</a> was absent. Although Descartes\' views were not universally accepted they became prominent in Europe and North America, allowing humans to treat animals with impunity. The view that animals were quite separate from humanity and merely <a href="/wiki/Machine" title="Machine">machines</a> allowed for the maltreatment of animals, and was sanctioned in law and societal norms until the middle of the 19th century. The publications of <a href="/wiki/Charles_Darwin" title="Charles Darwin">Charles Darwin</a> would eventually erode the Cartesian view of animals. Darwin argued that the continuity between humans and other species opened the possibilities that animals did not have dissimilar properties to suffer.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Historical_impact">Historical impact</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ren%C3%A9_Descartes&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Historical impact">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Emancipation_from_Church_doctrine">Emancipation from Church doctrine</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ren%C3%A9_Descartes&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Emancipation from Church doctrine">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:DescartesMeditations.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="500" data-file-width="345" decoding="async" height="319" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/61/DescartesMeditations.png/220px-DescartesMeditations.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/61/DescartesMeditations.png/330px-DescartesMeditations.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/6/61/DescartesMeditations.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:DescartesMeditations.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Cover of <i>Meditations</i></div></div></div>\n<p>Descartes has often been dubbed the father of modern <a href="/wiki/Western_philosophy" title="Western philosophy">Western philosophy</a>, the thinker whose approach has profoundly changed the course of Western philosophy and set the basis for <a href="/wiki/Modernity" title="Modernity">modernity</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Russell2004p516_94-0"><a href="#cite_note-Russell2004p516-94">[94]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[95]</a></sup> The first two of his <i>Meditations on First Philosophy</i>, those that formulate the famous <a href="/wiki/Cartesian_doubt" title="Cartesian doubt">methodic doubt</a>, represent the portion of Descartes\' writings that most influenced modern thinking.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[96]</a></sup> It has been argued that Descartes himself didn\'t realize the extent of this revolutionary move.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[97]</a></sup> In shifting the debate from "what is true" to "of what can I be certain?," Descartes arguably shifted the authoritative guarantor of truth from God to humanity (even though Descartes himself claimed he received his visions from God)&#8212;while the traditional concept of "truth" implies an external authority, "certainty" instead relies on the judgment of the individual.\n</p><p>In an <a href="/wiki/Anthropocentrism" title="Anthropocentrism">anthropocentric</a> revolution, the human being is now raised to the level of a subject, an agent, an <a href="/wiki/Emancipation" title="Emancipation">emancipated</a> being equipped with autonomous reason.
